As the second year of my administration begins, we con-
tinue to be guided by the theme, “Ve-he yay Berakkah - - and
Be Thou a Blessing”. This theme is handed down to us by our
parent body, Women’s League for Conservative Judaism.
Women’s League was founded more than half a century ago
by Mathilde Schechter. It has grown and progressed from a
handful of Sisterhoods to an international organization which
encompasses of 800 Sisterhoods with a membership in excess
of 200,000 women.
Our own Sisterhood boasts of the largest membership in
the state of Florida.
Through our varied programs such as Jewish Home Beau-
tiful we were able to show our members how to beautify their holiday tables. The
Show and Kvell program gave each member a chance to make Hanukah decorations
and to “show-off” their own handcrafts and family treasures. “Financial Confidence
For Today’s Woman” was the theme for our paid up membership meeting. These
very informative programs such as “Consumer Fraud And Rip-Offs In Our City”
were well received and enjoyed by everyone in attendance.
The Sukkot Celebration, Hanukah Fair, Furim Carnival, Simhat Torah and other
holiday celebrations met with great success. The Donor Fashion Show and Torah
Fund Luncheon and Flowers For Pesach And Rosh Hashanah added to a most
enthusiastic and successful year.
I look forward to a year of continued support and encouragement from my board
and Sisterhood members.
Shalom,
Annetta
BOARD MEMBERS: L-R; Isabel Balotin, Annetta Kornblum, Horty Gendzier, Shirley
Bielski, Kay Schneider, Shelly Kulchin.

Since the publication of the last Center Yearbook, the Jacksonville Jewish Fed-
eration has come into being. The old format of the Jacksonville Jewish Community
Council has been changed and the Federation is now comprised of a cross section
of delegates elected from the community at large to the Board of Directors. The
new format will provide a more effective way for the community to deal with the
complex issues that face us in the latter 1970’s and 80’s.
The Federation will continue to provide services to the community as in the past,
and will embark in new directions that will increase the quality of Jewish life in
Jacksonville.
The Federation will continue to conduct the annual Combined Jewish Appeal
which raises monies for our local agencies as well as the United Jewish Appeal and
national organizations. The Federation has made a major increase in its commit-
ment to both our local Jewish Family & Children’s Services and the River Garden
Hebrew Home for the Aged. The Federation recently added to its staff, Susana
Rothstein, who will be the new Director for Programs within the Jewish com-
munity. Susana will be responsible for myriad programming and planning. The
Jewish community, through the Federation programs, will make a major effort
toward providing services in those areas where little has existed in the past - - areas
such as Senior Citizen Programming, Single Parent Family Programming, College
Age Programs, Jewish Singles in their 30’s and 40’s, etc.
The Federation provides a new vehicle for service to the Jewish community and
it is the hope of the Officers and Board of this newest of Jacksonville organizations
that we have taken a major step toward unifying our Jewish community for the
betterment of all individuals and organizations within our Jewish community.

ference of Conservative Sisterhoods.

Fund in our Sisterhood. L-R; Karen Mizrahi, Ginette Mizrahi, Sue Becker,

COMMUNITY
“Alone a Jew is nothing. But if he is with other Jews,
he’s a force. Because then automatically he inherits
all the strengths and all the tears, all the despairs
and all the joys of his ancestors. A Jew alone
cannot be Jewish. A Jew can be Jewish only if
he’s part of the community.”
—Elie Wiesel

HA ? IRAY1M
Do you want to have fun? Do you want to really enjoy yourself? How about meeting some new friends?
Would you like to learn to disco, to square dance, to become a connoisseur of fine wines or a dealer at
blackjack, or even learn the secrets behind the Ha’Rayim hot dog? If yes is the answer to all of these
questions, Ha’Rayim is the group for yop. Become active in Ha’Rayim and we promise you all of this and
more.
Ha’Rayim originally started out as the young family fellowship of the Jacksonville Jewish Center.
However, since we are all now four years older and due to the popularity of Ha’Rayim, we now have active
participants of all ages. And, why not? We’re still having a great time.
This year, for instance, we had our third annual Poor Man’s Raffle with a live D.J. and scrumptious
buffet. We also assisted in the very successful Shusan Casino night. On a more religious note, various
Ha’Rayim couples hosted Friday evening services.
And, let us not forget our famous Ha’Rayim/Day Camp picnic. The picnic is held each year in celebra-
tion of Lag Bomer. The whole family comes out to enjoy softball, volleyball, basketball, games and water
slides for children, and our delicious Ha’Rayim hot dog. In fact, the softball
game was so successful that the games continued throughout the summer.
The purpose of our group is not fund raising. We just provide a way for Jewish
couples to get together in a relaxed social atmosphere. We have fun as we share
wholesome Jewish experiences.
We would love to have you join us. Bonnie & Neil Sandler
Ha’Rayim Presidents
Bonnie and Neil Sandler
Bauurat Qa Misbimba
Havurat Ha-Mishpacha, a group of young Center families who have organized to plan
religious, educational and cultural experiences, have planned a very exciting year. The
year began with a Shabbat experience. The group met for afternoon Minha services and
then assembled for a tasteful Shalosh Seudot filled with singing of old and new Israeli and
Hassidic songs. The program concluded with a very beautiful and creative Havdalah and
a pleasant exchange of Shavua-tov, a good week. Any young families interested in inten-
sifying their Jewish commitments and personal growth through education, religious ob-
servances and Jewish experiences please feel free to participate.
An open invitation is being extended to all young families. You may call the Center office,
Rabbi David Gaffney or Dr. Neil Abramson for more information.

Boasting of the longest continuous existence of any social club in the Jewish community, the member-
ship of the Mr. and Mrs. Club consists of just what its name implies. It was organized a quarter of a century
ago for the sole purpose of promoting fellowship, friendships and good times for married Jewish couples.
Dances are held every month to the music of live bands. Meals and snacks are served and each com-
mittee decides on its own type of entertainment. All of this is climaxed with a gala New Year’s dance
including a cocktail party, full course dinner and professional entertainment.
As this organization is non-profit, all of the aforementioned is enjoyed at a nominal cost. There is no
age limit and membership is open to the entire married Jewish Community. Couples who are interested in
joining may contact any officer or board member.

This is a year of celebrations for NCJW! Nationally, the organization recently celebrated its 85th birth-
day. We here in Jacksonville are proudly marking our 40th year of existance.
National Council of Jewish Women is an organization which, in the spirit of Judaism, is dedicated to
furthering human welfare in the Jewish and general community, locally, nationally, and internationally.
Our community service projects include: Pre-school vision testing to discover sight problems, service
to foreign born, the Art Enrichment Program, which trains volunteers at the Art Museum to bring and
discuss art in the public and private schools. In conjunction with the University of Florida and the Jewish
Federation, we provided Tay Sachs testing and education to the Jacksonville Jewish community. Our
Mental Health Forum, in coalition with the Mental Health Association, attracted over 2,000 participants.
A film of the Mock Trial, “Pursuit of Privacy”, produced by our Section is available for educational use
through the Haydon Burns Library. Our new project, just getting underway, is producing Talking Books
for use by the blind and handicapped.
In Israel, NCJW’s Educational Research Center is an innovative program for education of the dis-
advantaged. Fellowships for Israeli teachers and social workers who wish to study in our country and the
continued maintenance of our Hebrew University High School are all projects which are helping to educate
Israel’s youth.
NCJW’s legislative group, “Talk and Tour” and our advocacy programs have been educational for our
members and effective in promoting the Council’s concerns on a national, state and local level. A trip to
Tallahassee during the legislative session is available to all members and puts into action many of our
priorities as well as being an exhilarating experience.
Our meetings are held on the third Wednesday of the month. You are invited to contact any of the
officers regarding membership.

The attractive Library, newly furnished and equipped through the generosity of Mrs. Henry Kramer, will
be available for the congregation to enjoy. The facilities include the Tofield collection, the Solomon
Schechter Day School Room and a reading and browsing area.
The collection of books and periodicals will be expanded greatly to satisfy the reading needs of the
congregation. New and stimulating library related programs will be developed to encourage increased
use of the facility. Plans are being formalized to have the Library open at scheduled times.
In the past year the Library Committee joined with the Sisterhood in sponsoring a series of book reviews
and comments on current events as a part of the adult education program. Participants in the program
were: Mrs. Laurie Benwick, Mrs. Martin Wolf, Mr. Elliott Palevsky, Mrs. Lewis Meisel, Mr. Gene Korn-
blum, Mr. Alan Taffet, Mrs. Debbie Dreher and Rabbi David Gaffney.
We mourn the loss of our beloved member, Harold Hilsenrad of blessed memory. Harold’s dedication
and years of service is greatly appreciated and will be remembered for many years to come.
Minnie Schreiber
HARRY GENDZIER
On Sunday, October 29th at 7:30 p.m. the Jacksonville Jewish Center
State of Israel Bond Committee gave a Testimonial honoring Mr.
Harry Gendzier at which time he was presented with the coveted Israel
30th Anniversary Award for his dedicated and outstanding service
to the Jewish Community and to Israel. The guest speaker for the
event was one of America’s most distinguished journalists, David
Shoenbrun. We congratulate Mr. Harry Gendzier on achieving this
great honor, through his past and continuing service to the Jewish
community.

Photo 1 - Elliott Palevsky, Mr. Elliott Palevsky, the new Executive Director of River Garden
Photo 2 - Ballgame, River Garden residents and staff “Take Me Out To The Ballgame”
Photo 3 - Seder, In addition to full traditional Passover Sedorium, a special Seder included
the act of participation of the cronically disoriented residents. Reconnecting with tradition
is good therapy.
The River Garden Home is a vital, integral part of the Jacksonville Jewish Community. Through its
residential care program, River Garden serves community members who need a caring and socially sup-
portive environment, including medical and nursing care, in order to continue and extend meaningful
life activity. The short-term program serves those who need specialized care and rehabilitation in order to
return to community living.
In its 33 years of service, River Garden has become a natural part of our community’s life cycle. Its pre-
sence assures continuity of Jewish life style with continuing family and community ties to those for whom
home residential care is the best possible life alternative.
The Home is situated on the banks of the St. Johns River, surrounded by gardens. Its nursing and
psycho-social services are provided by a well trained staff. Although River Garden serves the frail, ill and
mentally impaired aged, it stresses the “wellness” of the individual while attempting to control the effects of
the illness they are suffering.
Through individualized plans of care, the Home tries to strike a living balance between institutional re-
quirements and individual needs and styles. This positive accent on living, with a variety of social, cultural
and recreational programs helps create the special climate and tone of River Garden. This is also trans-
mitted through the caring and encouragement of a professional and support staff of 180 people, providing
services 24 hours a day, seven days a week. The families of residents and the whole Jewish community,
through active involvement and support of River Garden, complete this living cycle.
This year marked the retirement of Sidney Entman, the remarkable founding Executive Director of
River Garden. The position is now held by Elliott Palevsky who is devoting himself to “continuing River
Garden’s dynamic role within the Jewish community by meeting the changing needs of the special popu-
lation it serves.”

ATID
Our children are very important to the future of Judaism.
Once our children go to college we want them to know that
their synagogue still cares about them.
This year Rabbi Gaffney and Rabbi Kentof directed some of
their energies to establishing new lines of communication with
our college students. News was sent to them about Israel
and the American Jewish Community. Readings in Jewish
thought, holiday packages, Sabbath candles and wine were
also sent.
Through this effort the Center tries to convey to our young
adults who are away from home that the Jewish experience
can take place anywhere.
The reaction to the “care” packages was fantastic. Corres-
pondence is received daily from the recipients. This will con-
tinue for many years to come.
During winter, spring and semester breaks the group has
been getting together for Shalosh Seudot, discussions and
socials. Below are pictures of a recent canoe trip taken by
ATID members.
If you are of college age or are a single adult, call Rabbi Dov
Kentof at 268-6736.
===== PAGE 42 =====
r
What are the 3
most widely held securities
in the United States?
U. S. Savings Bonds,
A. T. & T. . . . and
STATE OF ISRAEL BONDS!
You can’t argue with success, as the saying goes. And Israel Bonds
have had a lot of success since their inception in 1951. Over 350
of the largest banks and financial institutions in the United States own
Israel Bonds. Almost $4 billion worth have been sold, of which $2 billion
has been repaid as they came due.
State of Israel bond money never leaves the United States. The
proceeds remain here in the form of bank credits, permitting Israeli
industrialists and agricultural experts to purchase equipment and
machinery to create jobs for newcomers to Israel, who are arriving
by the hundreds each week.
There are dozens of ways you can use Israel bonds other
than in your investment portfolio . . .
Gifts to Universities: every major college in the United States has
accepted Israel Bonds. If you choose Harvard, they’ll match your gift
with an equal Israel Bonds purchase.
Use Israel bonds instead of cash for travel in Israel.
Pension Plans: Many pension fund and Keogh Plan retirement funds
use Israel Bonds as investments.
A reception honoring Sidney Entman will be held in October at the
Jacksonville Jewish Center.

In an attempt to serve the entire congregation, a Sukkah Mobile was erected on a
flat bed truck. Parents and youth of the congregation spent many hours working on it,
under the supervision of Rabbi Dov Kentof. The Sukkah Mobile traveled all over the
city bringing the spirit of Sukkot to River Garden, Mount Carmel and to our members’
homes and sister congregations.

ZIONIST ORGANIZATION OF AMERICA
JACKSONVILLE DISTRICT
President AlanTaffet
Vice Presidents
Membership Cantor A. Marton
Public Relations Joel Bressler
Programming Dr. Milton Mann
Projects Emanuel Ehrlich
Education Leonard Selber
Treasurer AnnShmunes
Recording Secretary Laurie Benwick
Corresponding Secretary Lillian Meizlik
Chairman of the Board Rabbi Gary Perras
BOARD MEMBERS: Jack Becker, Hyman Selber, David Goodman, Mike Dunay, Dr.
Nelson Goldman, Dr. Sam Rosenthal, Johnthan Shlackman, Dr. Michael Greenburg,
Dr. Chiel Wind, Rabbi Dov Kentof, Nathan Shmunes, Abe Fages, Allen Cohen and Robert
Neid.
P O BOX 23780
JACKSONVILLE. FLORIDA
32217
Founded in 1897 the Zionist Organization of America has been a moving spirit toward the establishment and maintenance of a
free, secure and independent State of Israel. To be a Zionist is to be committed to Israel and the future of the Jewish people.
On April 29,1979 Z. O. A. sponsored a gala banquet for the Jewish National Fund to honor the memory of Neal F. Portnoy
and a grove will be planted in his name. We also note with sadness the passing of our beloved member, devoted Zionist and
ardent worker for Z.O.A., Irvin B. Rothstein.
The President hails the peace treaty with Egypt and acknowledges the great leadership and fortitude of Prime Minister
Menachem Begin. We pray for a lasting peace in the Middle East and hope the American government will be even handed in this
issue.
Commit yourself to the preservation of Jewish identity and dignity everywhere by becoming a member of Z.O.A. Any Z.O.A.
officer will provide you with membership information, or call Cantor A. Marton at 641-7093.
1979 marks the 100th birthday of Albert Einstein. He was not only the physicist who confidently that space and time were not
what they seemed to be, but he was a passionate pacifist who blossomed out as a standard bearer for Zionism.
It is noted in a book by Ronald W. Clark that Einstein was one of the greatest contradictions of our time; the German who
hated the Germans; the pacifist who changed his mind; the ambivalent Zionist who was asked to hear the Israeli state; the
physicist who believed in God.
In Germany after World War I, the forces supporting assimilation were certainly strong, but so too were Einstein’s feelings
once he had become seized of the Zionist cause. Just how strong is shown by his letter of April 3, 1920, refusing to attend a
meeting to help combat anti-Semitism in academic circles.
“I should gladly come if I believed it possible for such an undertaking to succeedhe wrote.
First, however, the anti-Semitism and the servile disposition among us Jews in our own ranks would have to be combatted
by more knowledge. More dignity and more independence in our ranks! Not until we dare to regard ourselves as a nation,
not until we respect ourselves, can we gain the esteem of others, or rather only then will it come of its own accord. There
will be anti-Semitism in the sense of a psychological phenomenon as long as Jews come into contact with non-Jews —
what does it matter? Perhaps we owe it to anti-Semitism that we can maintain ourselves as a race. I at least believe so.
If I catch sight of an expression like “German citizens of Jewish faith” I cannot help smiling a little sadly. What is there
to be found in this pretty label? What is Jewish faith? Does there exist a kind of unbelief by virtue of which one ceases
being a Jew? There is not. But it suggests that the right people believe two things, i.e., (1)1 don't wish to have anything to
do with my poor (East European) Jewish brethren, and (2) I do not want to be taken for a child of my own people, but only
as a member of a Jewish communtiy.
Is this sincere? Can the “Aryan” feel any respect for such underhand fellows? I am neither a German citizen, nor is there
anything in me which can be designated as “Jewish faith”. But I am a Jew and am glad to belong to the Jewish people,
even if Ido not consider them in any way God's elect. Let us calmly leave anti-Semitism to the non-Jew and retain our love
for people of our kind.
I hope that you will not frown on account of this confession. No harm or unkindness is meant.
Einstein was later to put his point of view even more pungently. “The German Jew who works for the Jewish people and for
the Jewish home in Palestine no more ceases to be a German than the Jew who becomes baptized and changes his name ceases
to be a Jew,” he wrote in 1926. “The two attachments are grounded in realities of different kinds. The antithesis is not between
Jew and German, but between honesty and lack of character. 241

P.T.A. is a vital part of the Solomon Schechter Day School. Day School parents have cooked and served
delicious, nutritious lunches this past year under the supervision and hard work of Harriet Brodsky. Phono-
graphs and physical education equipment have been purchased as a result of a very successful magazine sale
under the direction of Shirley Bielski. Sharon Witten kept our checking and savings account in order. A Book
Fair, chaired by Shirley Kollin, and a winter vacation activity time coordinated by Shirley Bielski helped to add
to our savings account. Judy Mizrahi helped all of our children observe the various holidays by making the
necessary purchases and preparations. Many of us attended a very interesting “Seminar on Nutrition” and one
on “Effective Parenting”, thanks to Sue Alterman. Isabel Balotin kep us all informed by sending out newsletters
and doing all of our publicity. Our children became leaders, organizers and workers in their Student Council
with help from Phyllis Levin and Suzi Roey. Carolyn Krestul notified all officers and home room mothers of our
board meetings and Marilyn Mass kept marvelous minutes of all that went on at the meetings. Sandy Shapiro,
our hospitality chairwoman was always there with delicious refreshments. This year the 5th and 6th grades
participated in a Sex Education program. This was well presented and well received by students and the parents
who previewed and reviewed it.
Many thanks to the many parents who helped in our various endeavors. Without your time, energy, psycho-
logical and financial support, we could not have accomplished so many projects. The staff of both the school
and the Center were always supportive. My thanks to you all - - I’ve loved the past two years.
Rhoda Goldstein, Outgoing P.T.A. President

===== PAGE 66 =====
71
Dr. Irving Greenberg
One of the major highlights of the Center’s calendar
year was the extraordinary Shabbaton weekend
held April 20-22. Sponsored jointly with the Com-
munity Council (Federation), we had as our guest
“Scholar-in-Residence” Dr. Irving Greenberg, who
is the Director of the National Jewish Conference
Center. Dr. Greenberg spent a full weekend with us
lecturing on contemporary Jewish issues. On
Sunday Rabbi Greenberg spoke at the opening of
the Holocaust Museum. The entire community
participated in the day’s events.
Presented by The Jacksonville Jewish Center
and the Jacksonville Jewish Community Council

History and Activities
The members of the burial society used to attend at the
bedside of the dying person and receive his final declaration
or confession of faith (Viddui). They would wash and dress
the corpse, conduct the burial service, and finally, together
with the observance and performance of the traditional
rites, conduct the actual burial itself. In the case of the poor,
the burial society performed its services without any
charge. In all cases, however, whether there is a charge or
not, the members of the society fulfill their task as a sacred
duty or obligation (Mitzvah) which confers merit upon all
who take part therein. Since respect and acts of charity
shown to the dead were regarded by the Jews in all ages as
the highest form of charity and human service, the
members of the Hevrah Kaddisha have always been highly
respected in the Jewish communities; in many cases in the
past they were accorded special social and religious
privileges in return for their important and unselfish service.
The actual preparation of the dead for burial,
undertaken by the Hevrah Kaddisha, is known in Jewish
law as “taharah” (cleansing, purifying). During this Taharah
definitely prescribed ceremonies are observed,
Communal fraternities for the purpose of providing
burial and its attendant rites for the dead have existed
among the Jews ever since the Talmudic period. The
Hebrew term for such a fraternity is Hevrah Kaddisha (Holy
Society).
As soon as the society is notified by the local funeral
home of a Jewish death, out of respect for the dead, a
Shomer or watchman is notified and sits with the body,
especially at night, until the time of the funeral.
The practices of cremation, speeding up the
decomposition of the body, or embalming, the slowing
down the process, flowers at the funeral or public viewing of
the body are all discouraged by Jewish custom both
universally and locally.
The Jacksonville Hevrah Kaddish was formed in
January, 1974, and has been functioning ever since. It
consists of about twenty men and women who perform the
necessary tasks on a rotating basis. Anyone who would be
interested in becoming a member of the local society may
contact Joel Bressler, its President, for further information.
of Hevrah Kaddisha
accompanied by the reading of specific prayers. The body is
washed from head to foot in lukewarm water, dried and
then clad in the burial clothes (Tachrichin). During all this
ceremony the members of the society preserve a proper
gravity and respect. To prevent funerals from becoming
very expensive burdens upon the survivors Rabbon
Gamaliel 1, in the latter part of the First Century, ordered
that he be buried in a simple linen garment, and this
procedure was soon adopted by the great masses of the
Jewish people. Men are in addition wrapped in the same
Tallit (prayer shawl) which they have worn at services
during their life but with one of the fringes removed. As soon
as the body is fully clothed in the Tachrichin, it is placed in a
simple wooden coffin, again in an effort to provide
uniformity in the treatment of the dead and to prevent
excessive cost of burial.
If one wishes to honor their dead by purchasing a more
expensive casket, they are encouraged instead to give
the additional money to the deceased’s favorite loved one’s
charity.

TORAH READERS
Rabbi Akiba tells us in Ferek: “Beloved are Israel for to them was given a precious instrument, the Torah. It is by special divine love that they
are informed of this.” To Rabbi Akiba, love is greater when properly expressed. The Jewish people have obeyed his dictum. The Torah, the
Bible, became their portable fatherland. By its spirit they were able to live in an idealistic and dignified fashion, despite the vicissitudes of
fortune.
There are a number of ways of “knowing” the Torah; understanding its content, being able to translate it, being able to render its public
reading. This last approach has opened a wonderful opportunity for continuous education. A large percentage of our Bar Mitzvah candidates
during the past generation read the weekly portion at their Bar Mitzvah ceremonies and have continued to learn new portions. To these have
been added knowledgeable laymen, staff members, fathers of Bar Mitzvahs and a number of adults who have taken special training. The result
is that today we have a roster of several dozen readers who can master the intricacies of reading without vowels, punctuation or musical
tropes for one or more Aliyahs on Sabbaths, holidays and week-days. These people, of all age-levels, form a very positive nucleus of the con-
gregation for intensive participation and leadership. The readers are listed on the front page of the Bulletin each week. Some are identified
under the pictures on these pages. Many more have joined this exclusive group during the past year. As the current Bar Mitzvah candidates
complete their training, they too will join the ranks. We hope all of them will go ‘Mi-Chayil el Chawyil’, from strength to strength, achieving
additional skills to round out an entire year of great performances.
Ted Gross
L-R; Jeff Harris, Hal Baumgarten, Ted Gross, Dorone Manasse, Charles
Krestul, Blair Selber, Yale Balotin.
L-R; Jeff Morris, Neil Abramson, Ted Gross, Abe Bielski.

Lawrence Rackow
Executive Director
Founded in 1917 as the United Jewish Charities, J. F. C. S. had a long and glorious history as the Jewish
Welfare Society. It was professionalized in 1948 and adopted its current name in 1962. Its goals were and
are to help individuals and families lead happier and healthier lives, to enable individuals to approach
their maximum potential and to strengthen and/or restore family functioning and stability. In 1978, 466
individuals were served in one or more of its programs. Since its professionalization in 1948, more than
7000 families or individuals were served.
Its main program is to provide casework and counseling service to individuals and families. It is a licensed
child placing agency. It has reach-out programs at the State Hospital and at Union Correctional Insti-
tution. It provides special services to senior citizens and does the local admission studies for River Garden
Hebrew Home. It provides short term financial assistance to local and transient families and individuals.
With funds from the Jewish Federation it is responsible for the local resettlement program.
To supplement the casework program, the staff and its president started and lead a club for isolated
persons, an over 40 singles group, a discussion group at Mt. Carmel and two clubs at River Garden.
Responding to the opening of the gates from the U. S. S. R., the Community more than doubled its quota
for community resettlement from 9 in 1978 to 21 by September, 1979. This was made more dramatic as the
grant was submitted in April, having the 21 arrive in a five month period. Hopefully, the door will remain
open and the 1980 quota will be much greater.
Judy Mizrahi is serving as coordinator. The response of the Community to Judy Mizrahi’s call and example
has been magnificent. More volunteers and furnishings came in than ever before. The volunteers have been
having deep meaningful experiences. Units of five volunteers have been raised for each family arriving,
with back up forces of translators, teachers, drivers, doctors and dentists, etc. They have formed a most
efficient and enthusiastic team. The already settled New Americans are among the most energetic and
effective volunteers. All four synagogues have opened their doors to the newcomers. The Jewish Center
can be very proud of its camp and school scholarships and one year free memberships offered to the
newcomers.

TORCH
RUN
The First Annual Torch Run took place at the Jacksonville Jewish Center
on December 24, 1978. It was carried by the members of our various religious schools
and youth groups, from St. Augustine Road to San Jose Boulevard. The torch was then used
to light the Menorah, made by Rabbi Dov Kentof. This huge menorah was erected in front of
the Center to symbolize the light of freedom and hope for our people. A very moving
ceremony and candlighting program ensued. Members of the Board of Directors,
Sisterhood, youth groups, each lit a candle. Judge Louis Safer spoke about
“Freedom For All People”. The blessings were chanted by Raymond Cohen.
Each day of Hanukah, before Minyan, one flame was lit until eight candles burned.
Photo 1. Over 200 people turned out to participate in the ceremony.
Photo 2. Judge Lou Safer is thanked by Chairman of the Board, Bud Shorstein.
Photo 3. Rabbi Dov Kentof explains the Torch Run to Day School students.
Photo 4. One branch of menorah is lit by Renee Gordon, who represented Sisterhood. Rabbi Dov Kentof gives her a hand.
Photo 5. Jeff Presser passes torch to Jodi Roey.
Photo 6. Jill Goldstein and Stacey Sena have a turn.

CUB SCOUTS PACK 14
CUB SCOUTS
Back row L-R; Adam Jacobson, Bruce Gelman, Com-
mander Sidney Engelhardt. Middle row; Darren Rohan,
Arnie Harrison, Colman Brodsky, Michael Paul, Bobby
Greenspoon, Michael Gordon, Randy Figur, Steven
Shapiro, Ricky Plotkin, Chip Abolafia, Joel Nied, Adam
Panken, Kenny Haft. Front row; David Klein, Stephen
Zehngut, Scott Freedman, Ross Jeneff, Jonathan Wein-
traub, Dennis Mizrahi, Shaun Usoff, Richie Davis.
Cub Scouting as an introduction to Boy Scouts plays an impor-
tant role for boys between the ages of 8 and 10. The boys advance
from Wolf to Bear and then on to Webelos.
In this day and age when there are so many temptations, our
boys find themselves interested in completing their achievements
toward a higher rank, and participating in the monthly pack
meeting. There they put on skits and show off their crafts. One
very exciting meeting presented this year was “An Evening of
Magic” where all Scouts were taught magic tricks and had a
chance to present them to the Pack.
During the year, they share many new and different experi-
enees. They look forward to the annual Pinewood and Rocket
Derbys, Scout Sabbath, and the Blue and Gold Banquet.
To close the year, members of Pack 14 and their families
gathered at the Center Campsite for a day of father-son relay
races and cooking over an open fire. The highlight of the event
was the presentation of the Larry Hacker Cub Scout Award.
It went to the Cub with the best attendance at Den and Pack
meetings, achievements and good will. The recipient for 1979
was Joel Nied. An individual plaque was presented to Joel and his
name has been added to the permanent plaque which remains at
the Center.
Marilyn Panken,
Den Mother Leader

ADULT STUDIES
Rabbi Gaffney along with the Adult Education Committee began a most ambitious Hebrew Literacy
Campaign and adult study at the Jacksonville Jewish Center.
The Hebrew Literacy was committed to make basic reading of Hebrew a skill for every adult. Classes
were available every day, morning and evening. A staff of professional and skilled lay people instructed
these classes in a pre-determined format.
The Adult Education classes included Jewish Ethics and Values, Hebrew Folk Dancing, Basic Jewish
Skills, Major Forces in Jewish History, and study groups. The Sisterhood Bat Mitzvah Class continued
through its second year and is now preparing for a Bat Mitzvah in the fall.
The Sisterhood and Center Library Committee co-sponsored Library Shabbatonim. The purpose
was to bring to the Jewish Community, current books, and periodicals through the medium of book
reviews, debates and panel discussions.
The film series included such films as “The Fixer” and “The Apprenticeship of Duddy Kravitz” and
more. Every aspect of the Adult Study was a success. We thank Rabbi Gaffney for all the work he did to
expand our Jewish knowledge.

As an added bonus a two story , 13 room house was situated
on the front of the property. It still stands today and is
used by the Center Day Camp.
"The Land Acquisition Committee was
formed under the co-chairmanship of Sam
Spevak, Dr. Neal Roth and Alex Freund.
Sam Spevak had been very active in the
real estate business and synagogue leaders
were depending on his expertise to pull
them through. He did not let them down.”
"My recollection is that I met with
Louis Safer who said the Center was looking
for a new site”, Spevak said. "It just so
happens that a few years previous to this I
had been in the house building business
and we ran down every piece of property
in Duval County that was five acres or larger.
At Spevak’s disposal were maps of the
entire Duval County area he had used in
his own business. The search for land
began.
Several pieces of property were consid-
ered. Among them were an 11 acre tract
owned by Ed Ball of Old St. Augustine Road
and a 33 acre parcel right on Goodby’s Lake,
in the vicinity of where the Beauclerc Bay
Apartments now stand. The latter was ruled
out, Spevak said, because the land was too
low.
"I looked at a lot of property, but there
was one piece that looked like the best
thing for the Jacksonville Jewish Center”,
Spevak said.
The land was a 35.8 acre parcel just off
State Road 13. As an added bonus, a two
story, 13 room house was situated on the
front of the property. "The purchase price
was about $75,000”, Spevak recalls, "But
the house was worth between $20 - 25,000.
You knock off the value of the house and we
were paying about $50,000 for the land,
or a little over $1,000 an acre.”
An added consideration at the time of the
land search was the Center Day Camp,
thriving under the leadership of activities
director Robert Fischer. The camp had
everything going for it with one exception -
a permanent site. Whether renting a pool at
a local motel or renting campgrounds in
Switzerland, the day camp was faced with
the problem of* available resources. Given
these considerations, the Mandarin property
site became irresistible.

253
ORT of Jacksonville
ORT, the Organization for Rehabilitation through Training, is an international organization which for
a century has brought freedom to world Jewry. ORT operates in 30 countries with 76,500 students learning
90 trades in 800 training units. They represent people gaining an education and a future career in archi-
tectural drawing, telecommunications, avionics, hotel management, machine design, computer techno-
logy and more. Special programs are available for the physically handicapped, the aged, and language train-
ing programs for immigrants bound for English speaking countries. Different courses for different people
with different needs and qualifications are offered.
Jacksonville Area Council helps support the ORT program and consists of four Chapters each meeting
at different times to serve the needs of all the women in the community. The Area Council is the body which
services the four ORT chapters.
The following are the officers of the Area Council and the four chapters and their meeting days:
Afternoon Chapter President - Marsha Pollock (Meets 2nd Monday afternoon each month)
Beauclerc Chapter President - Sandy Miller (Meets 1st Friday morning each month)
Evening Chapter President - Cathy Kunsberg (Meets 1st Tuesday evening each month)
Riverfront Chapter Officers to be elected (Meets 1st Tuesday evening each month)
ORT AREA COUNCIL: L-R; Seated, Joanne LeDoyen, V.P. Community, Anita Wars-
choff, President; Terry Roshin, V.P. Membership; Karen Backilman, V.P. Honor Roll;
Standing L-R; Chris Arab, Financial Secretary; Sue Ann Blumenthal, Corresponding
Secretary; Sylvia Davidson, Parliamentarian; Ida Drayman, Treasurer; Not pictured,
Penny Marks, V.P. Education; Betty Gray, Recording Secretary.

Patronize Our Advertisers — They Deserve Your Support
They tell of Adam:
How frightened he must have been when
for the first time
he saw the sun disappear,
ending the light of day.
It was Adam’s first darkness!
How could he understand the night,
when he had never seen a dawn ?
After the splendor of the sun,
how astonishingly dark the darkness was;
how desperate the long terror of the first fall of night...
until Adam learned day would come again:
that there is order in the universe.
And he could begin to see
how much light remains in the sky at night:
the stars, and their enduring promise of the sun.
The returning star of day.
Adam learned
the night is never wholly dark, and no night is endless...
even as each of us must learn it
in our own times of trouble and of darkness.
The light is never far.

Rabbi Dov Kentof, with the help of
many parents, provided a very unique
Shabbat experience for our youth. The
beauty and atmosphere provided by
the outdoors enhanced the joy and
beauty of Shabbat.
Services, athletics, storytelling and
recreation were all part of the camping

This past year B.B.G. has done very well, we’ve had plenty of activities. Everything from Ice and Roller-
skating to a trip to Disney World. This past December we had our annual No-Talk-A-Thon which raised
$300.00 for the March of Dimes. We’ve had creative services and a surprise breakfast that all turned out
very well. Our biggest event of the year was our Sweetheart Heart Throb Banquet and Dance.
Many plans have been made for the coming year. It is very hard to get in touch with the girls in Jackson-
ville. So, if you are a girl who is in the 8th through 12th grade and you’re interested in having a great time,
please contact Mona Schaffer, 731-0204, or Juli Shapiro, 733-1219. We would love to have you.

AZA Officers
This year AZA has proved to be Jacksonville’s finest young men’s or-
ganization. After a slow start, AZA managed, through the dedication of its
members, to accomplish many fine projects and programs.
Realizing that the treasury was low the group set out to fund-raise. This
years raffle winners were, Rose Lodinger, Rabbi Dov Kentof and Margie
Treece.
Jacksonville’s AZA and BBG hosted a leadership convention, where
young men and women from all over Florida came to learn the skills of
leadership, and to make important plans for the year. “Sweetheart
Hearthrob” was the best ever. The group turned the Beauclerc Country
Club into a tropical paradise. While dinner was being served, live enter-
tainment was provided by Rocky and Renee Morris. The highlight of the
evening was when Mona Schaffer was chosen as “Sweetheart”.

SYNAGOGUE — CENTER ADMINISTRATION
BLANCHE SLOTT
Activities Director
KENNETH BOWDEN
Comptroller
FLORENCE ROBIN

ESTHER FISHLER

Religious Services — Traditional prayer services are held
daily, morning and evening in the Witten Chapel. Friday
Family Services, Sabbath and Holiday Services are observed
throughout the entire year.
Personal Religious Observances — Traditional Jewish
customs are observed in connection with Circumcision,
Naming of Girls, Pidyon Ha-ben, Bar Mitzvah, Bat Mitzvah,
Confirmation, Marriages, Conversion to Judaism,
Bereavement.
Education — Daily Kindergarten and Nursery with
outstanding program of secular and religious content.
Hebrew School, carefully graded with intensive Hebrew
content, including Jewish history and customs, Sunday
School for kindergarten, first and second grades: Solomon
Schechter Day School, a graded secular and Hebrew
School, 1st through 6th grades; Hebrew High School with
Pre-Jr. and Junior Congregations and Minyanaire.
Extension Services — Library, outstanding collection of
juvenile and adult Jewish books; Adult Education, religious
and cultural Jewish courses; music programs of religious and
cultural Jewish music; P.T.A.; Sisterhood and Ha’Rayim, a
fellowship of young families; social activities provided by Mr.
and Mrs. Club, Single Adults; Family Recreation Program
(Olympic pool), summer weekdays and Sundays for all ages.
Youth Activities — ATID for college age youth; United
Synagogue Youth, various age levels are served by Center
Youth League, Charles Palot Club, Kadima, Boy Scouts,
Cub Scouts, Girl Scouts, Brownies; Sunday Funday;
athletics, programs for youth and adults; Center Day Camp
and summer program at our 36 acre site on San Jose Blvd.
Family Recreation Program — Center Campsite . . . swim-
ming, athletics, snack bar; available for members during the
summer months.
For additional information, call the Center office — 268-6736.

Progressive Credit Union

William E. Papet, Chairman

The Progressive Credit Union was established, and is operated to serve the financial needs of its members consisting of men
and women of the Jacksonville Jewish Community.
In 1977, we assisted many Jacksonville businessmen and families with loans for automobiles, appliances, marriages, and other
worthwhile personal needs. Under a special program designed to aid students with College tuition, 32 Jewish students were
assisted in their higher education. The Student Loan Program is fully guaranteed by the U. S. Government.
All members receive free Life Insurance on their savings, up to $2,000.00. All loans are covered with free Credit Life Insurance
for the full outstanding balances. With all of our extra services rendered, the ratio charged for interest is lower than that of other
lending institutions in the area. All Savings are insured up to $40,000.00 by the Florida Credit Union Guarantee Company.
We extend a cordial invitation for all to join with us in the Progressive Credit Union to build a stronger Jewish Community
in Jacksonville. For further information, please call Mr. Joe Cohen at 354-4770.

for Jewish Ladies
Seated L-R; Nathan Gross, Max Becker, Milton Hecht,
David Gradman, Joe Cohen, Moe Safer, Standing L-R;
Irwin Gendzier, W. Z. Goldberg, Herman Gelman, Herman
Paul, Stanley Gelman, Eugene Henry.
Organized in 1960, Cassia Garden Circle, an affiliate of the Garden Club of Jacksonville, is a group of
Jewish women who are interested in gardening. Many of the charter members are still active in the group.
Cassia is a group of tropical trees, shrubs and plants with compound leaves, yellow flowers and long pods,
found in Israel. The Garden Club meets monthly. Programs have included Japanese Flower Arranging,
bonsai, hanging baskets, underwater arrangements, potpourri and landscaping. Many exciting plans have
been made for next year’s programming. Spend an interesting morning, and enjoy a light lunch with some
pleasant ladies. If you are interested call Isabel Balotin at 268-3362.

OUR NEW CENTER SYNAGOGUE
Still Halfway to our Bridge
We are still running to the end of the way,
We are still doing our best to reach the Aleph-Bet.
We are always smiling to the fire boiling,
We shake hand to hand as the boy growns.
We look far away to the sunshine on the east,
To Jerusalem with love ever and ever, joy and peace.
How much the mother will enjoy her baby when he is in the cradle.
Later on he will enjoy with brother and sister.
Open your heart and ask the Lord to fill it up,
With joy, peace and love and high Sinai goes up.
The day will go quick as the dark
But God’s spirit always a shining spark
Let us enjoy Jerusalem and Mount Sinai.
To be grown and keep the treasure of my dear B’nai
Let us be strengthened from Heaven to defeat all the enemies
And idols in the valley, to say we are your epedemies.
By: Isaac M. Kabbani
Rosh Hashanah 5740

1979-80 OFFICERS
Mount Carmel Gardens
Tenants Association
Mary Foor
President
Rubin Janofsky

BOARD MEMBERS: Celie Falis, House Services ; Irene Erich and Ruth Blanc, Liason; Beatrice Yales, Sunshine; Frances Janofsky, Educa-
tional; Ruth Blanc, Bingo; Pauline Schemer, Newsletter; Kate Bandrimer, Outside Entertainment; Ethel Korn, Trip; Rose Altmayer, Tele-
phone; Fannie Kaufman, Music; Israel Schemer, Ex-officio; Milton Foor, Parliamentarian.
MOUNT CARMEL GARDENS Mount Carmel Gardens is a Senior Citizens home, seventeen
floors high, with 208 apartments, occupied by both Jews and Gentiles, all friendly, contented and congenial neighbors. It is conveniently lo-
cated, with transportation, shopping and religious organizations within walking distance. Each apartment has an emergency button which
brings immediate help to those needing it, each floor is equipped with fire and smoke alarms - - in short, it is a living community which provides
much more than housing.
We are very fortunate in having a very able and helpful office staff, in the persons of Mr. Andrew Johnson,* Manager, and Mrs. Donna Sor-
rells, Secretary, - - plus the maintenance crew, who are always on hand to do whatever needs fixing or correcting.
The tenants have formed an association, which is independent of management, the aims of which are to furnish entertainment, almost daily
varied educational activities (which, incidently, are available to any senior citizens wishing to take part), occasional bussing to trips and shows
for those who want to go. We are recipients of volunteer entertainment from throughout the city, and send our own Carmel Singers and
Carmel Players to anyone requesting us to entertain for them.
Our bi-monthly parties, our Saturday night dances and occasional dances with live orchestras, our twice-weekly Bingo, our invitations to the
Alhambra Dinner Theatre and to Theatre Jacksonville, the Christmas-Chanukah and New Years Eve parties, plus the individual apartment
gatherings and those in our lobby just to socialize, has earned us the reputation of being the “most fun” complex of our type in the city. With so
much going on, there is just no need for time to hang heavy, or for folks to be bored.
The flag pole and flag on the island in front of our building were recently donated and installed by the Jewish War Veterans, and the ground
around it was landscaped by Mrs. Rosalie Becker - - all honoring the memory of Frances Wilensky. Over the door of our meeting room is a
plaque, with the wording “The Frances Wilensky Auditorium”, also in her honor. This will always be a living memoriam for the one who was
responsible for our building’s initial concept, and who had done so much for our building and for us.
Summarizing, Mount Carmel Gardens is a high-rise filled with an enthusiastic and appreciative seventeen floors of people, who have made
many friendships, and are very happy to call their apartment “home” - - responding to the desires of retirees, for security, comfort, safety,
independence, and the enrichment of stimulating social-recreational activities shared with others.
Pauline Schemer
At our costume party - The Madame The mother and father of the bride at our “mock First Prize
(Bess Safer), The Dutch Girl (Lona wedding”. Costume Party

Doü K entof.
DÍRectOR of Education & Youth
Of course, you’re a hero to your children!! They love
and admire you, respect you, usually obey you - and above
all, imitate you.
Which, you see, is the rub. They imitate the wrong things
you do as well as the right things. If you aren’t careful about
right and wrong, in your own behavior, your children will
get confused. They’ll either think the wrong things are all
right, or they’ll lose their hero-worship for you.
Do you, in effect, tell your child, “you go get a Jewish education - but include me out!” or,
“you have to find time from play and public school for a Jewish Education - but I’m too busy
to get one!” or, “we’ll enroll you in a Jewish school, but we won’t give a darn about your atten-
dance, progress, cooperation, continuation!” or, “the teacher knows best, he’s always right -
you do what he says, but don’t expect me to!”
What a hero! You want your child to become smarter, more intelligent. But he imitates you -
remember? Learned anything new lately, yourself, in Hebrew, Jewish history, literature, art,
Yiddish, current events? Taken any new courses, read any new books? You want your child
to be a good Jew, or a better Jew, or a happier Jew. Setting him an example, are you? Making
you home and your daily life more Jewish? Taking more part in Jewish affairs? You want your
child to respect Jewish life and Jewish studies. All right, try showing some respect yourself!
Start his Jewish education early, keep him at it indefinitely. Visit the school, get to know his
teachers and his books, get active in the parents group. Try to give your child the kind of Jewish
environment his school is giving him.
When it comes to Jewish education, what kind of a hero are you?

Camp Ramah
The summer is the best time for learning
and growing. The Jacksonville Jewish
Center and the generosity of two'
families have made it possible for many
youngsters to attend Camp Ramah for a
totally Jewish experience in a very
relaxed and enjoyable environment
of a summer camp. Donations made
through Sisterhood and the Center
to the Myra Thier Lazarus Fund during
the year are also used for this purpose.
Ramah Campers - Back row L-R; Stephanie Gray, Melissa Roey, Joy
Pollock, Sabrina Katz, Jeff Presser, Jodi Roey, Horty Gendzier. Front L-R;
Stephanie Katz, Dennis Mizrahi, Simeon Abramson, Jonathan Katz,
Judity Abramson. Missing from picture Laura Abramson, Janet Jacobson,
and Gil Kentof.
Abe Selber established in his will a perpetual trust for
specific Jewish educational purposes. His children
Iris and Franklin, who have never lost their interest
in the Center, have made sure that the proceeds
of this trust have also been used to send campers
to Ramah. Leonard Selber is shown presenting the
Abe Selber Scholarship to Melissa Roey, Joy Pollock
Stephanie Gray and Jeff Presser.

===== PAGE 199 =====
Rabbi David Gaffney
Rabbi David and Inge Gaffney,
and their children
Asher and Shalva
Rabbi’s Message —
Dear Friends,
As we move together into the ’80s - the beginning of a
new decade - we become even more sensitive to the chai-
lenge, the hope and the promise of the future. What should
our perceptions be of our future as a congregation - a com-
munity of commitment?
Neither as individuals nor as a people are we simply the
sum total of our past experience; nor are we merely a col-
lection of our present encounters. We are what we would
be.
The character of a synagogue cannot be determined
only historically - by what we were; only sociologically - but
what we are. It is in great measure determined propheti-
cally - by what we would be.
To be a member of the Jacksonville Jewish Center ought
to mean that we are part of a larger Jewish Self - a col-
lective Self which has lived for forty centuries, and has
extracted from that experience the wisdom and the values
that can help us in life. I turn to this larger Self for my
interior life - to help us discover what is real, authentic;
what is important, what is sacred. We hope to gain from
the pulpit and from the class a vision that can help us inter-
pret the meaning and the purpose of our being. We expect
to derive from the service, from the sermon, from a lecture,
from a weekend, moral strength and spiritual morale -
stable anchors that can help us withstand the storms that
buffet us in life. To be a member of the Jewish Center ought
to mean that we are part of a caring congregation, a caring
Jewish community upon whom we can make claims for
help and who in turn can make claims on us.
To be a member of the Jewish Center ought to mean that
we can share with others the opportunity of growing
Jewishly and humanly. It means the presence of intimate
circles of family and friends, of Havurot with whom we can
experience the joys of Judaism, the celebration of festivals
and a discussion of the critical items on the agenda of
Jewish life. It means the opportunity to express friendships,
to live out the Jewish imperative of gemilat hasadim, of
compassion, of reciprocal relations of responsibility. It
means to experience Jewish warmth in a depersonalized
society.
To be a member of the Jewish Center ought to mean
that there are Jews willing to help us overcome our doubts,
our fear and ignorance of Judaism. We need Jews to be
Jewish. We need Jews to become Jewish - to know how
and why and when to celebrate our people’s personal and
collective sacred moments, to know how and why to pray
and sing the insights of our tradition.
What ought we be? The Center should be a microcosm
of Jewish civilization, in its programs and lectures, in its
philosophy and in its ethics.
To be a member of the Center ought to make a critical
difference to my being. Our synagogue is created by, for
and of Jewish people and it is rooted in the reality of God,
Torah and Israel.
Together with God’s help, and by our will, we can be-
come a community of commitment, of hope - to renew the
old and to sanctify the new.
Inge, Asher and Shalva join me in wishing you and yours
a year of health and of happiness, a year of joy and glad-
ness, a year of purpose and fulfillment.
Rabbi David Gaffney

The First Annual Alexander Brest Tennis Tournament
was played this year.
Twenty-six mens teams and 12 ladies teams participated.
Each player received a T-shirt designed by George Strum-
lauf. The winners were Bud Shorstein and Leo Jacobson,
Wilbur and Bennie Margol and Ann Shorstein and Florence
Haft. Each winner was presented with a trophy. Sincere
thanks to the Tournament Committee which consisted of
Ronnie Pollock, Chairman, Sherwin Burman, Arthur Gold-
stein, Stephen Jerard, Shelley Kulchin and Marsha Pollock.
We thank these contributors for their support.

The merriment and all age participation of the holiday of Purim can be
felt in the Synagogue while the Megillah is being read. Always a capacity
crowd, the holiday creates a joyous atmosphere the children remem-
ber from year to year. The sound of the grogger and dangling jewelry,
beards and mustaches, three-cornered hats all add to the excitement.

The Holocaust Museum was prepared by the
9th graders of the Judaica High School of the
Jacksonville Jewish Center. Under the guidance of
Rabbi Dov Kentof, the museum depicted the catas
trophic period in our history.
Why, you ask, must we have a Holocaust
Museum? Can’t we just “forgive and forget?”
If we do “forgive and forget” then we, as today’s Jews, are sinning
against ourselves. Only from history can we begin to understand
and control our present and future. History cannot be repeated; so, we
must not ever “forgive and forget. ” We must inspire the present
generation of youth to help build a world where genocide could not,
once again, occur. We must always remember the physical and moral
fiber of a people that resisted the machinery of destruction against
overwhelming odds. And, finally, we must realize the weapon of
Anti-Semitism, and how the Nazis wielded this too to create a
Holocaust.
Holocaust
MUSETJ
Recovered ceremonial objects on display.
9th GRADE — HEBREW HIGH SCHOOL
These students were responsible for the museum. Back row
L-R; Ian Mann, Perry Kantor, Laura Abramson, Scott Marko-
witz, Bill Meisel, Jeff Presser, Ron Katz. Second row L-R; Jodi
Roey, Michelle Honigman, Beth Jawitz, Joy Pollock. Missing
from picture; Greg Winarsky, Judy Sprecher, Rabbi Dov
Kentof.
The entire community was invited. Rabbi Irving Greenberg and Rabbi David
Gaffney explain to Mayor Jake Godbold the
significance of the Star of David and the
Yahrzeit lamp.

Hadassah
Hadassah, the largest Zionist organization in the world, as
well as the largest women’s voluntary organization in the
United States, has a membership of 350,000 members who
dedicate themselves to help Israel, a developing democracy,
to help itself.
The Jacksonville Chapter has approximately 900
members and consists of three active groups: TIKVA, which
meets during the day; HANNAH SENESCH, the evening
business and professional women’s group; and SHIR-LI, the
young women’s group which also meets in the evening.
The President’s Column
Shalom,
Leadership in Hadassah prouides women with the
opportunity to share in the historic upbuilding of Israel
and thus enrich their own lives through service and
education.
Women for whom material comforts and possessions
are not life’s highest goals associate themselves with a
cause through work with an organization. No society in
history has had the variety and multiplicity of organ-
izations which are to be found in the United States.
Everyone receives many appeals for membership in
organizations. Fortunately, vast numbers of women
have chosen Hadassah as the focus of their service.
Indeed , many have made it a central force in their lives.
Hadassah is more than an association of Jewish
women. It is a unique organization, a vital constituent
of the world Zionist Movement. It has made a major
contribution to American civilization and to the Jewish
people.
Leadership in Hadassah implies two things: the pri-
vilege of standing at the helm of a great living movement
in Jewish history , and the obligation to absorb and
communicate to others the wealth of knowledge, ideas,
and ideals embodied in this movement.
Fondly,
Joan Waitz
Chapter President

River Garden Auxilliary
OFFICERS
President Mrs. Martin Wolf (Mary)
First Vice President Mrs. Herbert Lewitt (Maxine)
Second Vice President Mrs. HymieFialkow (Charlotte)
Third Vice President Mrs. Henry Halpern (Julie)
Financial Secretary Mrs. Mitchell Peltz (Lois)
Corresponding Secretary Mrs. Karl Frisch (Michelle)
Recording Secretary Mrs. Roy Brooks (Barri)
Executive Secretary Mrs. Raphael Klepper (Doris)
General Treasurer Mrs. Milton Greenspan (Vivian)
Parliamentarian Mrs. Sidney Entman (Rose)
Mary Wolf - President
Guided by our motto “Service with a Heart” the River Garden Auxiliary has dedicated itself for over
50 years to serving the needs of our older citizens. Times are changing fast, and we of the Auxiliary must
keep pace to meet the every increasing new needs in order to maintain the current excellent standards of
River Garden Home for the Aged:
As nearly as possible we want to keep River Garden as a home away from home for our dear friends
and relatives. We have something for everyone. The budding artist, the dramatic actress, the home gar-
dener and the creative writer can all find outlets for their talents at River Garden. The Hobby Shop, the
Resident’s Magazine and Project Greenthumb have been most successful this year.
The Auxiliary also sponsors the Golden Sunshine Club for both residents of the Home and other
senior citizens who wish to participate in their meetings which are educational as well as social.
Holidays are especially important to our residents and the Auxiliary plans parties throughout the year
on such occasions as Chanukah, Purim, Mother’s and Father’s Day add to their enjoyment.
The Auxiliary is proud to provide the extra budgetary needs for the maintenance of our excellent
facility. Our fund raising projects, Tombola, Donors, Social Printing, Tree of Life, Resident’s Memorial
Plaque, Life Membership, have enabled us to contribute to new furnishings, air conditioning, redecora-
tions, kitchen and infirmary equipment. Recently a new salad bar was purchased for the use of the residents
and staff.
At River Garden there is plenty of opportunity for the volunteer who wants to lend a helping hand.
Friendly Visitors, Adopt a Friend and the Red Cross welcome volunteers who will write a letter, go shop-
ping or just give a little comfort and cheer to a resident.
With the coming New Year we hope to increase our services through a new intensive volunteer
program. Our main purpose is to make our residents as comfortable and happy as possible.
On behalf of the Auxiliary we wish you much “naches” for the coming New Year.

Message From The President
Laurie DuBow
As we close the book on the year 5739, it would seem to be an appropriate time to crystallize our
thoughts on where we will, or could possibly be, going in 5740.
There is no question about things being uncertain. They are uncertain, but I submit to you they have
been, and are always uncertain to some extent. The mood of the economy is up or it is down depending
on what leadership is saying and doing... And so it is in the synagogue; we react to optimism in a favorable
manner ... we believe in those who are leading us.
We are pleased with fellow congregants who have the desire and capability to continue the reputation
of our Synagogue Center as a leader of the community. The names are too numerous to mention here, but
they have given us the support and involvement needed to maintain our programs and facility. These
people have been a great asset to me ... I could not function or continue to serve you without their help.
I am enthused about what happens at our Synagogue, and most importantly I am confident your
Officers and Board of Directors know who and what we are ... where we are ... and where we are going.
Don’t weep for “the good old days”. Speaking for myself, “the good old days” were not all that good
anyway, whereas today isn’t all that bad . . . and tomorrow will be only as good as you and I make it.
Linda, Susan, Michael and I send our best wishes to you for a Happy and Healthy New Year.
Laurie DuBow
The DuBows, Susan, Laurie, Linda and Michael.

Jewish Women’s
Bowling League
The Jewish Women’s Bowling League has been
in existence for 20 years. Bowling began at
Plaza Lanes and is presently held at Bowl-A-
Rama. The league is open to all Jewish women
of the community and all are invited to join.
Bowling is every Tuesday morning beginning
at 9:30 a.m. The only requirement is that you
establish a bowling average. For information
call Bea Caplan at 744-2607. Spend a delight-
ful morning with some very charming and
vivacious ladies.
PAST AND PRESENT OFFICERS: Back row L-R; Flo Ber-
nick, Past President; Joan Marón, Sargeant-at-arms; Ellie
Wildstein, Incoming Vice-President; Clarice Shift, Treasurer;
Front L-R; Bea Caplan, Outgoing President; Shirley Cohen,
Incoming Secretary; Lois Robins, Outgoing Secretary; Simmie
Benjamin, Sunshine Lady. Missing from picture, Incoming
President, Marilyn Freedman.

For 20 years the Jewish Men’s Bowling League has been a welcome “night-out” for the guys. Bowling is

Labor Day. Call Henry Weintraub at 733-1280.

Jewish War Veterans
LT. MEYER LIEBOVITZ POST 199
Jacksonville, Florida
Meyer Leibovitz Post 199, Jewish War Veterans honored former
W. A. V.E. Frances Wilensky (Deceased) by erecting a flag pole and
memorial plaque at the Mount Carmel Gardens, Mt. Carmel Ter-
race, just off University Blvd., on June 3, at 11:00 a.m. The first flag
to be flown is a flag that has flown over the U. S. Capitol, Washing-
ton, D.C.
Mrs. Wilensky, one of the early enlistees into the W.A.V.E.’s
attained the rank of Chief Petty Officer, U. S. Navy, before her
discharge. She became active in civic and veterans affairs becoming
a member of Post 199 immediately after her discharge. She was
instrumental in the construction and operation of the Mt. Carmel
development and saw it through to completion. One of her many
dreams was to see a flag of the United States fly over this complex,
however, her dream was never fulfilled due to other committments
of money. The Jewish War Veterans have now completed her
dream for her with this dedicatory ceremony. She, through the help
of Congressman Charles Bennett, procured the flag that will be
flown at the ceremony on June 3rd.
Accepting the honor were her sons Daniel and Billy Wilensky, who
then transferred the responsibility of the flag pole to the Board of
Directors of Mt. Carmel.
Mrs. Wilensky is the first woman to be honored and Post 199 Jewish
War Veterans is grateful to all those who have contributed their
money and time to make all this possible.
Anyone interested, please call A1 Sinoff at 744-5671.
Commander A1 Sinoff and Billy and Danny Wilensky.
Flag pole and memory plaque honoring
Fran Wilensky.

This has been a banner year for the A.B. Weil Lodge. We have been involved in many successful community projects. We participated in the
Bell Ringer campaign for the Salvation Army at Christmas, a C.P.R. mannequin was donated to the Red Cross, convalescent cards were sent
to Jewish patients in local hospitals. We gave an award for outstanding service to a local policeman. Lastly, and most important, as our main
project for the year, we are donating a Torah to the Hillel House at Florida State University.
An important step in lodge growth was achieved with the merger of the Wolfson Lodge with our lodge this year. We are anxious to affiliate
all interested Jewish men in our fine organization.
Much of the success of this year was due to our President, Milton Hecht and the fine group of officers who worked closely with him. We
expect the upcoming year to be even more successful and once again urge all Jewish men to join and become active.

GIRL SCOUT TROOP
162
Girl Scouting is more than a club, it is a nationwide, worldwide
movement for girls ages 7-17. Girl Scouts make a promise and
live by the Girl Scout laws. These simple guides prepare a young
girl to live in our bewildering modern world.
GIRL SCOUT PROMISE
On my honor, I will try;
To do my duty to God and my country,
To help other people at all times,
To obey the Girl Scout laws.
Troop 162 has fulfilled all of the obligations of scouting throughout
the year under the leadership of Clara Mann. Arts and crafts
camping, working on all badges has made some very worthwhile
experiences. Together the girls worked on many badges such as
troop camping, cooking, active citizen, which included a tour of
the Police building.
The troop had a great time bowling once a month and is making
up their own badge for it. Our troop also takes pride in being the
third top salesgirls for the annual cookie sale.

The Jacksonville Jewish Center takes great pride in its Daily Minyan. Through
the efforts of the captains and co-captains we have minyans every morning
and evening.
The minyan is of the utmost importance for many of our members who wish to
pray daily in the synagogue and for those who have yahrzeit or are bereaved.
Members of the Jewish community come to depend on the Center’s minyan.
The Center would like to thank all of the men who give of their time and to the
Captains and Co-captains who devote endless hours calling men to “make a
minyan”.

MINYAN
Mnyanaire
“And thou shalt bind them for a sign upon thine hand, and they shall be for
frontlets between thine eyes.” Deuteronomy 6:8 (Performing the mitzvah
are L-R; Sol Gol, Lester Greenfield, Norman Moss and A1 Sinoff.
Why does God in his
infinite wisdom require
Ten Men to hold a
Minyan?
If there are only five
books of the Torah
why can’t five
compose a Minyan?
If seven is a lucky
number, why can’t
seven meet to pray?
If the individual is
most important why
can’t one pray for
all?
Doesn’t God know that
Ten is hard —
It’s too early — too late
too far to go.
MINYAN AIRE PROGRAM
The Jacksonville Jewish Center Minyanaires are dedicated to a program
of prayer services, comraderie als well as social and intellectual stimulation.
A year of forums on contemporary social and religious issues which affect
the Jewish community and the community at large are being planned.
As part of its service to the health needs of the congregation, the Minyan-
aires in co-ordination with the American Heart Association, sponsor C.P.R.
classes (Cardiac-Pulmonary Resuscitation), and Blood Bank Drives.
This Sunday morning program begins with minyan at 9:00 a.m. in the
Witten Chapel, followed by breakfast and the speaker of the day. For infor-
mation call Chairman, Gene Kornblum — 737-6127.

how difficult it is
to raise a group
often?
Wouldn’t it be easier
to obey the commandments
with less?
But then of course God
knows that anything easy
loses its meaning —
And without the struggle
of getting ten —
The Daily Minyan would
come to its end.

===== PAGE 254 =====
"Everything came our way in time” — Sam Spevak
by Bruce Horovitz and Isabel Balotin
R arely does a synagogue in America have an opportunity
to be "ahead of the times”. For the Jacksonville Jewish
Center, the opportunity came 16 years ago.
It was on August 9,1963 that the Center purchased 35.8 acres
of land in an undeveloped part of town known as Mandarin, or
the "boondocks” as it might have been referred to back then.
Today, the synagogue sits on that property, right in the
middle of perhaps the most rapidly developing section of
Jacksonville.
Whether it was insight, luck, or more likely a combination
of both, the Jacksonville Jewish Center can proudly boast of
having been ahead of the game, ready to provide its program of
the future in a location that has become synonymous with
growth and progress in our community.
The selection of the State Road 13 property was no accident.
To those who were involved, the process meant long hours of
hard work.
There are few written records available to recount events
leading up to the purchase, but certain facts stand out in the
memory of those involved.
"We had been meeting at the Center on Third and Silver
and a lot of people in the community were saying that we ought
to move," recalls Wilbur Margol, a member of the Board who
was later to become Center President. "I remember one meeting
in particular that was very vocal, and as a result of that meeting,
we appointed a committee that was best qualified to find us
a good piece of property.”
Sam Spevak
"I looked at a lot of property, but there
was one piece that looked like the best
thing for the Jacksonville
Jewish Center.”

CEMETERY COMMITTEE
OF THE
Jacksonville Jewish Center
The Cemetery Committee provides a very necessary function of the Jacksonville Jewish Center. It is very important that
the Cemetery grounds are well kept and provide a peaceful resting place for our loved ones.
Our Cemetery Committee is co-chaired by Joe Becker and Jack Becker. Serving on their committee are Max Becker,
Herman Bloom, Eugene Henry, Hymie Selber, Alan Taffet, Sumner Waitz and Dr. Joe Witten.
Mr. Kenneth A. Bowden, Controller of the Jacksonville Jewish Center, also serves as general manager of our Cemetery. It
is his responsibility to oversee the upkeep and maintenance of the grounds. He has an excellent working relationship with Mr.
Jennewine who is general manager of Evergreen Cemetery. Mr. Jennewine has been very generous with his time and his
staff, Mr. Campbell, Mr. Miller and Mrs. Phillips. They have all taken excellent care of the Center’s property, aboe and beyond
what is expected. We appreciate everything they do.
Johnny B. Mercer has been custodian of our Cemetery for over 19 years. Mr. Bergeron of Southern Monument Co., has
always been extremely helpful to us too. We are very grateful to all these people who have given so generously of their time.

“ALL OF THE COMMUNITY OF ISRAEL IS BOUND — ONE TO THE OTHER“
The Talmud
The following thoughts are presented by the Portnoy Family with the hope that it will nuture a
concern for our most precious possession - the Jewish community.
We are all familiar with the Biblical story of Abraham bargaining with God to save the wicked cities
of Sodom and Gomorrah from destruction. After much perseverance on Abraham’s part, the
number of righteous people sufficient for saving the cities was reduced to ten. But ten were not to be
found - and the cities were destroyed.
The rabbis asked “Was it really possible that in the large cities of Sodom and Gomorrah ten
noble souls could not be found?” They suggested therefore that there were ten good men in the city
however, since they lived for themselves, were unconcerned with their community and exerted
no beneficial influence for the public good, they were not judged righteous men. The cities were
destroyed.
What an insightful and valid description of the source of Jewish strength, past and present - our
interdependence. The muscles and sinews which give us our real strength are the bands of to-
getherness which made of the Jewish people a community. The welfare of the “whole” was deemed
so important throughout our history that tradition suggests that the community of Israel is equal in
importance to the God of Israel and the Torah of Israel. Moreover, one could not properly achieve
Godliness in one’s life unless one recognized the needs in the face of one’s fellow man. Israel, the
Bible says, did not receive the Torah at Mt. Sinai until they became a community, ready to receive
the Torah as one people.
The most classic imperative of Jewish responsibility is described by Hillel - “Al tifrosh min ha-
tzibur” . . . “do not separate yourself from the community”. This statement suggests a reciprocal
relationship. A community is diminished by the apathy of its individual members. This is especially
the case with the Jewish community, facing the numerous challenges and crises with which itis con-
fronted. But the process is reciprocal. Those who remain unconcerned about the greater good
of the whole, diminish not only the community but themselves. Whatever affirmation we make as
Jews, whatever sense of identity we profess, we know that our inner life requires a living relation-
ship to our community and the people of Israel. Without a Minyan, the traditional representation
of the community, the presence of God is denied the individual even though he is engaged in prayer.
A man who has separated himself from the community will according to the rabbis not share in its
consolation and joy. What is more they add, “A man who retires to his home and says, what have 1 to
do with the needs of the community, why should I heed their words, such a man destroys the world.
The time has come for us to build. We need to create a community concerned with the well being
of all. History teaches us that the Temple was destroyed because of the pointless enmity which
existed between members of the Jewish community in ancient times. Rabbi Abraham Kook, the
late chief rabbi of Israel, suggested that the Temple will not be rebuilt until pointless enmity is re-
placed by wholesome love - one for the other and one for all. The time has come to nuture such love.
East Coast Fruit Co.
LOUIS PORTNOY — WALTER PORTNOY
AND FAMILIES

YEARBOOK
1979-80 5740
V
Well another year has gone by and another yearbook compiled.
One has only to read the yearbook to see all that has happened at
the Center this past year. Activities for all ages ... a building that
was used every day of the week . . . and lots of happy faces, young
and old. If have tried very hard to include all the activities. Unfor-
tunately some were not photographed but nevertheless just as
successful. I look forward to another year of the same quality of pro-
grams and activities. It seems to me that the Center gets better
each year.
I want to thank my friends, the Ghelerters and Fay at Mendelsons
for all their help and patience. A book of this size and quality takes
a lot of hard work and co-ordination ... but you too made it possible,
by your support and interest in the Center - that makes the differ-
ence. From my family to yours - a very happy and healthy year.
_ ^ Shalom,
d/te (cover Isabel
The first time that I say the “Shalom” fire sign was at the conclu-
sion of the Kadima Convention. Created by Rabbi Dov Kentof the
sign is constructed of wire and wrapped in rags, then it is set aflame.
When lighted, the carefully formed Hebrew letters that spell shalom
come to life. How we wish that the peace we pray for will become a
reality. Maybe we shall see it in the year 5740. The light that shines
from the sign, creates an atmosphere of warmth felt by all onlookers.
For one brief moment a feeling of absolute peace is felt when you
view it. We wish to thank Rabbi Dov Kentof for this magnificent
creation. We also wish to thank George Strumlauf for the design
of this years cover and Don Morris for the excellent photography
displayed on this cover.

Solomon Schechter Day School
r
L.
The Solomon Schechter Day School has a bicultural
curriculum of Hebrew learning and General Studies. The
highly structured yet individualized program in General Studies
has proven to be an outstanding education. The students have
continued to score high in achievement tests and are con-
tinuing to experience success in their classwork after leaving
our school. The classes are particularly impressive in terms of
Hebraic and Judaic accomplishment. The upper classes are at
the highest Judaic achievement level in the history of the Day
School.
The Solomon Schechter Day School of the Jacksonville
Jewish Center provides all Jewish children in the community
an outstanding General Studies education and a rich and
rewarding Hebrew-Judaic education. We are extremely proud
of our Nursery, Kindergarten and six grades. Our teachers and
the Education Committee of the Center have worked hard and
should be credited with the excellent progress of the school.
Marilyn Sandler, Principal
Marilyn Sandler
Principal Solomon Schechter Day School
Evelyn Borstein, School Secretary and Suzy Roey,
Assistant to the Administrator of the Supplemental
Schools.
